Mar 2019</li><li class="metadata__item">4<!-- --> Min Read</li></ul><a class="primary-button social-icon social-icon--linkedin" target="_blank" href="https://www.linkedin.com/cws/share?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fthreat-intelligence%2Fwhitefly-espionage-singapore&amp;token=&amp;isFramed=true">Share<svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" fill="none" class="linkedin_svg__responsive-icon" viewBox="0 0 48 48"><g clip-path="url(#linkedin_svg__a)"><path fill="currentColor" d="M44.447 0H3.544C1.584 0 0 1.547 0 3.46V44.53C0 46.444 1.584 48 3.544 48h40.903C46.407 48 48 46.444 48 44.54V3.46C48 1.546 46.406 0 44.447 0M14.24 40.903H7.116V17.991h7.125zM10.678 14.87a4.127 4.127 0 0 1-4.134-4.125 4.127 4.127 0 0 1 4.134-4.125 4.125 4.125 0 0 1 0 8.25m30.225 26.034h-7.115V29.766c0-2.653-.047-6.075-3.704-6.075-3.703 0-4.265 2.896-4.265 5.887v11.325h-7.107V17.991h6.826v3.13h.093c.947-1.8 3.272-3.702 6.731-3.702 7.21 0 8.541 4.744 8.541 10.912z"></path></g><defs><clipPath id="linkedin_svg__a"><path fill="currentColor" d="M0 0h48v48H0z"></path></clipPath></defs></svg></a></div></header><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>In July 2018, an attack on Singapore’s largest public health organization, SingHealth, resulted in a reported 1.5 million patient records being stolen. Until now, nothing was known about who was responsible for this attack. Symantec researchers have discovered that this attack group, which we call Whitefly, has been operating since at least 2017, has targeted organizations based mostly in Singapore across a wide variety of sectors, and is primarily interested in stealing large amounts of sensitive information.</p>

<p>Whitefly compromises its victims using custom malware alongside open-source hacking tools and living off the land tactics, such as malicious PowerShell scripts.</p>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><h2>Whitefly’s targets</h2>

<p>From mid-2017 to mid-2018, Whitefly launched targeted attacks against multiple organizations. While most of these organizations were based in Singapore, some were multinational organizations with a presence in Singapore.</p>

<p>To date, Whitefly has attacked organizations in the healthcare, media, telecommunications, and engineering sectors.</p>

<h2>How Whitefly compromises its victims</h2>

<p>Whitefly first infects its victims using a dropper in the form of a malicious .exe or .dll file that is disguised as a document or image. These files frequently purport to offer information on job openings or appear to be documents sent from another organization operating in the same industry as the victim. Given the nature of disguise, it’s highly likely that they are sent to the victim using spear-phishing emails.</p>

<p>If opened, the dropper runs a loader known as <a href="https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2019-013008-4820-99">Trojan.Vcrodat</a> on the computer. Whitefly has consistently used a technique known as search order hijacking to run Vcrodat. This technique takes advantage of the fact that Windows does not require an application to provide a specific path for a DLL that it wishes to load. If no path is provided, Windows searches for the DLL in specific locations on the computer in a pre-defined order. Attackers can therefore give a malicious DLL the same name as a legitimate DLL but place it ahead of the legitimate version in the search order so that it will be loaded when Windows searches for it. Whitefly frequently delivers Vcrodat as a malicious DLL that has the same name as DLLs belonging to legitimate software from various security vendors. The group leverages search order hijacking to assure that its malicious DLLs will be executed. Targeting security applications could allow the attackers to gain higher privileges for the malware, since the vendor’s component may be run with elevated privileges.</p>

<p>Once executed, Vcrodat loads an encrypted payload on to the victim’s computer. The payload contacts a command and control (C&amp;C) domain. Whitefly configures multiple C&amp;C domains for each target. The payload sends system information about the infected computer to the C&amp;C server and downloads additional tools.</p>
</div><blockquote class="paragraph paragraph-quote prose"><p>Whitefly usually attempts to remain within a targeted organization for long periods of time—often months—in order to steal large volumes of information.</p>
</blockquote><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>Once the initial computer on the targeted organization’s network is infected with Vcrodat, Whitefly begins mapping the network and infecting further computers. In order to carry out this operation, it uses publicly available tools, including Mimikatz (<a href="https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2012-042615-3731-99">Hacktool.Mimikatz</a>) and an open-source tool (SHA2: 263dc5a8121d20403beeeea452b6f33d51d41c6842d9d19919def1f1cb13226c) that exploits a known Windows privilege escalation vulnerability (<a href="https://cve.mitre.org/cgi-bin/cvename.cgi?name=CVE-2016-0051">CVE-2016-0051</a>) on unpatched computers. The attackers rely heavily on tools such as Mimikatz to obtain credentials. Using these credentials, the attackers are able to compromise more machines on the network and, from those machines, again obtain more credentials.&nbsp; They perform this tactic repeatedly until they gain access to the desired data.</p>

<p>Whitefly usually attempts to remain within a targeted organization for long periods of time—often months—in order to steal large volumes of information. It keeps the compromise alive by deploying a number of tools that facilitate communication between the attackers and infected computers. These tools include a simple remote shell tool that will call back to the C&amp;C server and wait for commands, and an open-source hacking tool called Termite (<a href="https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2002-011710-0057-99">Hacktool.Rootkit</a>), which allows Whitefly to perform more complex actions such as controlling multiple compromised machines at a time.</p>

<h2>Additional malware used in selected attacks</h2>

<p>In some attacks, Whitefly has used a second piece of custom malware, <a href="https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2018-080818-0255-99">Trojan.Nibatad</a>. Like Vcrodat, Nibatad is also a loader that leverages search order hijacking, and downloads an encrypted payload to the infected computer. And similar to Vcrodat, the Nibatad payload is designed to facilitate information theft from an infected computer.</p>

<p>While Vcrodat is delivered via the malicious dropper, we have yet to discover how Nibatad is delivered to the infected computer. Why Whitefly uses these two different loaders in some of its attacks remains unknown. And while we have found both Vcrodat and Nibatad inside individual victim organizations, we have not found any evidence of them being used simultaneously on a single computer.</p>

<h2>Links to other attacks</h2>

<p>Some of the tools that Whitefly has used in its attacks have also been deployed in other targeted attacks outside Singapore.</p>

<p>Between May 2017 and December 2018, a multi-purpose command tool (SHA2: 7de8b8b314f2d2fb54f8f8ad4bba435e8fc58b894b1680e5028c90c0a524ccd9) that has been used by Whitefly was also used in attacks against defense, telecoms, and energy targets in Southeast Asia and Russia. The tool appears to be custom-built and, aside from its use by Whitefly, these were the only other attacks where Symantec has observed its use.</p>

<p>In another case, Vcrodat was also used in an attack on a UK-based organization in the hospitality sector.</p>

<p>It's possible Whitefly itself performed these attacks but it’s more likely that they were carried out by one or more other groups with access to the same tools.</p>

<h2>Adept attackers with a large toolset</h2>

<p>It now appears that the SingHealth breach was not a one-off attack and was instead part of a wider pattern of attacks against organizations in the region. Whitefly is a highly adept group with a large arsenal of tools at its disposal, capable of penetrating targeted organizations and maintaining a long-term presence on their networks. Links with attacks in other regions also present the possibility that it may be part of a broader intelligence gathering operation.</p>

<h2>Protection/Mitigation</h2>

<p>Symantec has the following protection in place to protect customers against these attacks:</p>

<p><strong>File-based protection</strong></p>

<ul>
	<li><a href="https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2019-013008-4820-99">Trojan.Vcrodat</a></li>
	<li><a href="https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2018-080818-0255-99">Trojan.Nibatad</a></li>
	<li><a href="https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2002-011710-0057-99">Hacktool.Rootkit</a></li>
	<li><a href="https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2012-042615-3731-99">Hacktool.Mimikatz</a></li>
</ul>

targets\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eFrom mid-2017 to mid-2018, Whitefly launched targeted attacks against multiple organizations. While most of these organizations were based in Singapore, some were multinational organizations with a presence in Singapore.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eTo date, Whitefly has attacked organizations in the healthcare, media, telecommunications, and engineering sectors.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eHow Whitefly compromises its victims\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWhitefly first infects its victims using a dropper in the form of a malicious .exe or .dll file that is disguised as a document or image. These files frequently purport to offer information on job openings or appear to be documents sent from another organization operating in the same industry as the victim. Given the nature of disguise, it’s highly likely that they are sent to the victim using spear-phishing emails.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIf opened, the dropper runs a loader known as \u003ca href=\"https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2019-013008-4820-99\"\u003eTrojan.Vcrodat\u003c/a\u003e on the computer. Whitefly has consistently used a technique known as search order hijacking to run Vcrodat. This technique takes advantage of the fact that Windows does not require an application to provide a specific path for a DLL that it wishes to load. If no path is provided, Windows searches for the DLL in specific locations on the computer in a pre-defined order. Attackers can therefore give a malicious DLL the same name as a legitimate DLL but place it ahead of the legitimate version in the search order so that it will be loaded when Windows searches for it. Whitefly frequently delivers Vcrodat as a malicious DLL that has the same name as DLLs belonging to legitimate software from various security vendors. The group leverages search order hijacking to assure that its malicious DLLs will be executed. Targeting security applications could allow the attackers to gain higher privileges for the malware, since the vendor’s component may be run with elevated privileges.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eOnce executed, Vcrodat loads an encrypted payload on to the victim’s computer. The payload contacts a command and control (C\u0026amp;C) domain. Whitefly configures multiple C\u0026amp;C domains for each target. The payload sends system information about the infected computer to the C\u0026amp;C server and downloads additional tools.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"10:T1201,"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"\u003cp\u003eOnce the initial computer on the targeted organization’s network is infected with Vcrodat, Whitefly begins mapping the network and infecting further computers. In order to carry out this operation, it uses publicly available tools, including Mimikatz (\u003ca href=\"https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2012-042615-3731-99\"\u003eHacktool.Mimikatz\u003c/a\u003e) and an open-source tool (SHA2: 263dc5a8121d20403beeeea452b6f33d51d41c6842d9d19919def1f1cb13226c) that exploits a known Windows privilege escalation vulnerability (\u003ca href=\"https://cve.mitre.org/cgi-bin/cvename.cgi?name=CVE-2016-0051\"\u003eCVE-2016-0051\u003c/a\u003e) on unpatched computers. The attackers rely heavily on tools such as Mimikatz to obtain credentials. Using these credentials, the attackers are able to compromise more machines on the network and, from those machines, again obtain more credentials.\u0026nbsp; They perform this tactic repeatedly until they gain access to the desired data.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWhitefly usually attempts to remain within a targeted organization for long periods of time—often months—in order to steal large volumes of information. It keeps the compromise alive by deploying a number of tools that facilitate communication between the attackers and infected computers. These tools include a simple remote shell tool that will call back to the C\u0026amp;C server and wait for commands, and an open-source hacking tool called Termite (\u003ca href=\"https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2002-011710-0057-99\"\u003eHacktool.Rootkit\u003c/a\u003e), which allows Whitefly to perform more complex actions such as controlling multiple compromised machines at a time.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eAdditional malware used in selected attacks\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn some attacks, Whitefly has used a second piece of custom malware, \u003ca href=\"https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2018-080818-0255-99\"\u003eTrojan.Nibatad\u003c/a\u003e. Like Vcrodat, Nibatad is also a loader that leverages search order hijacking, and downloads an encrypted payload to the infected computer. And similar to Vcrodat, the Nibatad payload is designed to facilitate information theft from an infected computer.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eWhile Vcrodat is delivered via the malicious dropper, we have yet to discover how Nibatad is delivered to the infected computer. Why Whitefly uses these two different loaders in some of its attacks remains unknown. And while we have found both Vcrodat and Nibatad inside individual victim organizations, we have not found any evidence of them being used simultaneously on a single computer.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eLinks to other attacks\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSome of the tools that Whitefly has used in its attacks have also been deployed in other targeted attacks outside Singapore.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eBetween May 2017 and December 2018, a multi-purpose command tool (SHA2: 7de8b8b314f2d2fb54f8f8ad4bba435e8fc58b894b1680e5028c90c0a524ccd9) that has been used by Whitefly was also used in attacks against defense, telecoms, and energy targets in Southeast Asia and Russia. The tool appears to be custom-built and, aside from its use by Whitefly, these were the only other attacks where Symantec has observed its use.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn another case, Vcrodat was also used in an attack on a UK-based organization in the hospitality sector.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIt's possible Whitefly itself performed these attacks but it’s more likely that they were carried out by one or more other groups with access to the same tools.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eAdept attackers with a large toolset\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eIt now appears that the SingHealth breach was not a one-off attack and was instead part of a wider pattern of attacks against organizations in the region. Whitefly is a highly adept group with a large arsenal of tools at its disposal, capable of penetrating targeted organizations and maintaining a long-term presence on their networks. Links with attacks in other regions also present the possibility that it may be part of a broader intelligence gathering operation.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eProtection/Mitigation\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSymantec has the following protection in place to protect customers against these attacks:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFile-based protection\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cul\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2019-013008-4820-99\"\u003eTrojan.Vcrodat\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2018-080818-0255-99\"\u003eTrojan.Nibatad\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2002-011710-0057-99\"\u003eHacktool.Rootkit\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\t\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://www.symantec.com/security-center/writeup/2012-042615-3731-99\"\u003eHacktool.Mimikatz\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\r\n\u003c/ul\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of Compromise\u003c/h2\u003e\r\n"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"11:T53a,\u003cp\u003eNorth Korean state-backed attackers are now using the Medusa ransomware and are continuing to mount extortion attacks on the U.S. healthcare sector.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNorth Korea has long been involved in ransomware attacks and has been previously associated with the Maui and Play ransomware families. However, the Symantec and Carbon Black Threat Hunter Team has uncovered evidence North Korean actors using Medusa in an attack on a target in the Middle East. The same attackers also mounted an unsuccessful attack against a healthcare organization in the U.S.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMedusa, which is operated by the Spearwing cybercrime group, was launched in 2023 and is run as a ransomware-as-a-service, where affiliate attackers can deploy the ransomware in exchange for a percentage of ransom payments. More than 366 attacks have been claimed by attackers using Medusa.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnalysis of the Medusa leak site reveals attacks against four healthcare and non-profit organizations in the U.S. since the beginning of November 2025. Victims included a non-profit in the mental health sector and an educational facility for autistic children. It is unknown if all these victims were targeted by North Korean operatives or if other Medusa affiliates were responsible for some of these attacks. The average ransom demand in that period was $260,000.\u003c/p\u003e12:T2428,"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"\u003ch2\u003eHistory of extortion\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOne of the prime movers in mounting North Korean ransomware attacks in recent years has been the Lazarus sub-group Stonefly (aka Andariel). For many years, Stonefly was thought to be solely engaged in espionage attacks, particularly against high-value targets. However, the group became involved in ransomware attacks approximately five years ago. Its involvement in digital extortion came to public attention in July 2025, when\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/north-korean-government-hacker-charged-involvement-ransomware-attacks-targeting-us-hospitals\"\u003ethe U.S. Justice Department indicted a North Korean man named Rim Jong Hyok\u003c/a\u003e\u0026nbsp;on charges related to a ransomware campaign against U.S. hospitals and other healthcare providers. Rim is alleged to be a member of Stonefly, which is linked to the North Korean military intelligence agency, the Reconnaissance General Bureau (RGB).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe indictment shed some light on the motivation behind Stonefly’s move into ransomware. It alleged that the group was using the proceeds of ransomware attacks to fund its espionage activities, including attacks against the defense, technology, and government sectors in the U.S., Taiwan and South Korea.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe indictment, and\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.fbi.gov/wanted/cyber/rim-jong-hyok/download.pdf\"\u003ea $10 million reward for information on Rim\u003c/a\u003e, did not appear to deter Stonefly from mounting further attacks. In October 2024,\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.security.com/threat-intelligence/stonefly-north-korea-extortion\"\u003eour Threat Hunter Team found evidence\u003c/a\u003e of intrusions against three different U.S. organizations. Although no ransomware was successfully deployed, the attacks appeared to be financially motived since all victims were private companies and involved in businesses with no obvious intelligence value.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the same month, \u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/north-korean-threat-group-play-ransomware/\"\u003ePalo Alto Unit 42 reported\u003c/a\u003e that it had begun collaborating with the Play ransomware group.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttacker toolset\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eLazarus is using a range of tools in its current ransomware campaigns. While the TTPs – extortion attacks against the U.S. healthcare sector – are like previous Stonefly attacks, the malware tools used are not exclusive to Stonefly. For example, the Comebacker backdoor has previously been reported to be \u003ca href=\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2021/01/28/zinc-attacks-against-security-researchers/\"\u003eassociated with the Pompilus group (aka Diamond Sleet).\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eFew scruples\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe switch to Medusa demonstrates that North Korea’s rapacious involvement in cybercrime continues unabated. North Korean actors appear to have few scruples about targeting organizations in the U.S. While some cybercrime outfits claim to steer clear of targeting healthcare organizations due to the reputational damage it may attract, Lazaurs doesn’t seem to be in any way constrained.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eProtection/Mitigation\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor the latest protection updates, please visit the \u003ca href=\"https://www.broadcom.com/support/security-center/protection-bulletin\"\u003eSymantec Protection Bulletin\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of Compromise\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf an IOC is malicious and the file available to us, Symantec Endpoint products will detect and block that file.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFile indicators\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e15208030eda48b3786f7d85d756d2bd6596ef0f465d9c8509a8f02c53fad9a10 - Victims included a non-profit in the mental health sector and an educational facility for autistic children. It is unknown if all these victims were targeted by North Korean operatives or if other Medusa affiliates were responsible for some of these attacks. For many years, Stonefly was thought to be solely engaged in espionage attacks, particularly against high-value targets. However, the group became involved in ransomware attacks approximately five years ago. Its involvement in digital extortion came to public attention in July 2025, when\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/north-korean-government-hacker-charged-involvement-ransomware-attacks-targeting-us-hospitals\"\u003ethe U.S. Justice Department indicted a North Korean man named Rim Jong Hyok\u003c/a\u003e\u0026nbsp;on charges related to a ransomware campaign against U.S. hospitals and other healthcare providers. Rim is alleged to be a member of Stonefly, which is linked to the North Korean military intelligence agency, the Reconnaissance General Bureau (RGB).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe indictment shed some light on the motivation behind Stonefly’s move into ransomware. It alleged that the group was using the proceeds of ransomware attacks to fund its espionage activities, including attacks against the defense, technology, and government sectors in the U.S., Taiwan and South Korea.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe indictment, and\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.fbi.gov/wanted/cyber/rim-jong-hyok/download.pdf\"\u003ea $10 million reward for information on Rim\u003c/a\u003e, did not appear to deter Stonefly from mounting further attacks. This attribution was based on similarities in TTPs. After further analysis, we've concluded the payload used was Reynolds, an emergent ransomware family.\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA recent Reynolds ransomware campaign was notable because the ransomware contained a bring-your-own-vulnerable-driver (BYOVD) defense evasion component embedded within the ransomware payload itself.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNormally the BYOVD defense evasion component of an attack would involve a distinct tool that would be deployed on the system prior to the ransomware payload in order to disable security software. However, in this attack, the vulnerable driver (an NsecSoft NSecKrnl driver) was bundled with the ransomware itself.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBYOVD is by far the most frequently used technique for defense impairment these days. Generally, attackers will deploy a signed vulnerable driver to the target network, which they then exploit to elevate privileges and disable security software. Since the vulnerable drivers operate with kernel-mode access, they can be used to terminate processes, making them an effective tool for disrupting security measures. In most cases, the vulnerable driver is deployed along with a malicious executable, which will use the driver to issue commands.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile bundling a defense evasion component within the ransomware itself isn’t entirely novel, it is quite unusual and not what we typically see ransomware actors doing today. It was previously seen in a \u003ca href=\"https://www.fortinet.com/blog/threat-research/ryuk-revisited-analysis-of-recent-ryuk-attack\"\u003eRyuk ransomware attack in 2020\u003c/a\u003e, as well as an attack in which a \u003ca href=\"https://www.huntress.com/blog/obscura-ransomware-variant\"\u003elittle-known ransomware called Obscura was deployed in 2025\u003c/a\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eRecent activity\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe ransomware payload drops a vulnerable NsecSoft NSecKrnl driver and tries to create an NSecKrnl service. This driver is then exploited to attempt to kill processes. It targets the following processes:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"Sophos UI.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SEDService.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SophosHealth.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SophosFS.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SSPService.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SophosFileScanner.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"McsAgent.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"McsClient.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SophosLiveQueryService.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SophosNetFilter.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SophosNtpService.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"hmpalert.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"Sophos.Encryption.BitLockerService.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SophosOsquery.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"ccSvcHst.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SymCorpUI.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SISIPSService.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SISIDSService.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"SmcGui.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"sisipsutil.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"sepWscSvc64.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"MsMpEng.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"CSFalconService.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"cydump.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"cyreport.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"cyrestart.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"cyrprtui.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"cyserver.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"cytool.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"cytray.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"cyuserserver.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"CyveraConsole.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"tlaworker.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"ekrn.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"eguiProxy.exe\\t\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"egui.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"aswEngSrv.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"aswidsagent.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"AvastUI.exe\"\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\"ccSvcHst.exe\"\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe ransomware payload appends the “.locked” extension to files it encrypts.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe NSecKrnl driver is a Windows kernel-mode driver with a known critical security vulnerability (\u003ca href=\"https://nvd.nist.gov/vuln/detail/CVE-2025-68947\"\u003eCVE-2025-68947\u003c/a\u003e), which means that it fails to verify if a user has sufficient permissions before executing commands. This allows a local, authenticated attacker to terminate processes owned by other users, including SYSTEM and Protected Processes, by issuing crafted Input/Output Control (IOCTL)\u0026nbsp;requests to the driver.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlso of note in this attack campaign was the presence of a suspicious side-loaded loader on the target’s network several weeks prior to the ransomware being deployed. It is not certain if this was linked to the subsequent ransomware activity, but if it was it points to a long dwell time for the attackers.\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe GotoHTTP\u0026nbsp;remote access tool was also found on some machines on the target network the day after the ransomware was deployed. It is relatively unusual to see attacker activity on the victim network post ransomware deployment. This could be unrelated to the ransomware activity, but it could also point to an attempt by the attackers to maintain persistent access to the network, even after the ransomware was deployed.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eDefense evasion: A key step in ransomware attacks\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe impairment of defenses, usually by attempting to disable antivirus (AV) or endpoint detection and response (EDR) products, is a key part of ransomware attacks in 2026. Ransomware actors have added this step to their playbooks in a bid to evade detection prior to the deployment of a file-encrypting payload.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe use of impairment techniques and tools has risen markedly among ransomware actors over the past two years, most likely in response to vendors improving their ability to identify patterns of malicious activity that occur prior to ransomware deployment.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBy far the most frequently used technique for defense impairment is the BYOVD technique. Attackers will generally deploy a signed vulnerable driver to the target network, which they then exploit to elevate privileges and disable security software. These drivers are considered “vulnerable” as it should not be possible to leverage them in this way. A correctly written driver will contain safeguards to ensure they only respond to legitimate requests from authorized software. However, when these drivers fall into the wrong hands, they effectively become tools for privilege escalation.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBYOVD is popular with attackers due to its effectiveness and reliance on legitimate, signed files, which are less likely to raise red flags. A wide range of drivers have been used in such attacks, with anti-rootkit drivers developed by security vendors being among the most commonly exploited. Popular BYOVD tools frequently used by attackers include:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e0c8367ea73cdeb2a02bf106e91b6c970\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eTrueSightKiller:\u003c/strong\u003e A \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/MaorSabag/TrueSightKiller\"\u003epublicly available tool\u003c/a\u003e that leverages a vulnerable driver named truesight.sys.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e1773ac38adacb6ec8fca4b9be5a56550\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eGmer:\u003c/strong\u003e A\u003ca href=\"http://www.gmer.net/?m=0\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;rootkit scanner\u003c/a\u003e that can be used to kill processes.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"ea9d3cefb454fad18d034f0d30db94a9d\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWarp AVKiller:\u003c/strong\u003e A variant of a Go-based information-stealing threat called Warp Stealer, which appears to be just used to bypass security products. It uses a vulnerable Avira anti-rootkit driver to disable security products.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e635b5ff5f5109272eff2f81bf99b3e75\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eGhostDriver:\u003c/strong\u003e A\u003ca href=\"https://github.com/BlackSnufkin/GhostDriver\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;publicly available tool\u003c/a\u003e that leverages vulnerable drivers to kill processes.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e1dffa3fd3bc8f0d72244000ddc036062\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePoortry (aka BurntCigar):\u003c/strong\u003e A malicious driver\u003ca href=\"https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2024/08/27/burnt-cigar-2/\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;documented by Sophos\u003c/a\u003e that is frequently employed alongside a loader known as Stonestop. Unlike many drivers, Poortry may have been developed by attackers who then succeeded in getting it signed.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e7d3f3d9077af5102fed29e87fdd611fd\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAuKill:\u003c/strong\u003e A tool\u003ca href=\"https://news.sophos.com/en-us/2023/04/19/aukill-edr-killer-malware-abuses-process-explorer-driver/\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;documented by Sophos\u003c/a\u003e that uses an outdated version of the driver used by\u003ca href=\"https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/sysinternals/downloads/process-explorer\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;the Microsoft utility Process Explorer\u003c/a\u003e to disable EDR processes\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttackers do also leverage living-off-the-land techniques, using common Windows utilities, to disable security software, particularly Windows Defender. However, there is no doubt that BYOVD is the most common defense evasion tool we see used by ransomware actors today.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWill this tactic be adopted by more ransomware actors?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe question raised by this recent activity is whether we are likely to see this tactic be adopted by more ransomware actors and what advantages it might bring for them.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe advantages of wrapping the defense evasion capability in with the ransomware payload, and the reason ransomware actors might do this, may include the fact that packaging the defense evasion binary and the ransomware payload together is “quieter”, with no separate external file dropped on the victim network. It also may speed up the attack - if there is no gap between the defense evasion tool being deployed and the ransomware being dropped, there is no opportunity for defenders to stop the attack. In other scenarios, if defenders saw a suspicious driver being dropped on a system, they may have time to stop the attack before the ransomware is deployed.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEmbedding more capabilities into the ransomware payload itself may also help act as a unique selling point for ransomware developers who are attempting to attract affiliates. Having additional capabilities bundled with the ransomware payload may make ransomware attacks easier to carry out, as they would require less steps, potentially making such a payload more attractive to affiliates.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt will be interesting to see if more ransomware families begin embedding additional capabilities, such as defense evasion and others, in their ransomware payloads in the future.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eProtection/Mitigation\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor the latest protection updates, please visit the \u003ca href=\"https://www.broadcom.com/support/security-center/protection-bulletin\"\u003eSymantec Protection Bulletin\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of Compromise\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf an IOC is malicious and the file available to us, Symantec Endpoint products will detect and block that file.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e5213706ae67a7bf9fa2c0ea5800a4c358b0eaf3fe8481be13422d57a0f192379 – Suspicious file\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003ee09686fde44ae5a804d9546105ebf5d2832917df25d6888aefa36a1769fe4eb4 – Webshell – xxxxx.aspx\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003ebf6686858109d695ccdabce78c873d07fa740f025c45241b0122cecbdd76b54e – Loader – vspmsg.dll\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e6bd8a0291b268d32422139387864f15924e1db05dbef8cc75a6677f8263fa11d – Reynolds ransomware – wxt4e.exe, wxt4e.txt\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e206f27ae820783b7755bca89f83a0fe096dbb510018dd65b63fc80bd20c03261 – Vulnerable\u0026nbsp;NsecSoft NSecKrnl\u0026nbsp;Driver – 402.sys\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e230b84398e873938bbcc7e4a1a358bde4345385d58eb45c1726cee22028026e9 - GotoHTTP - gotohttp.exe\u003c/p\u003e"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"16:T6d5c,"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"\u003cul\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e5b8f15622a9603baadac43c81fcd7771\"\u003eMultiple tools used by the attacker bear hallmarks of having been developed using AI, such as detailed comments and numbered steps in scripts, and instructions to the attacker in debug messages.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e9eefdba5057b965b9744e0c6a9cf7d83\"\u003eAttacks begin with phishing emails, usually masquerading as job offers.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e9fd5628999ce2f68ac8931dac987db16\"\u003eAttacker is based in Vietnam and may be selling access to compromised organizations to other actors.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eA Vietnamese threat actor is likely using AI to author code powering an ongoing phishing campaign delivering the PureRAT malware and other payloads. The phishing emails masquerade as job opportunities and the attacker may be using them as a lure in the hope that recipients open the emails using work computers. The attacker’s usage of AI provides further evidence that the technology is being used by lower-skilled attackers to assist with developing tools and automating their attacks.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eEvolving attack chain\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe campaign,\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.trendmicro.com/en_us/research/25/l/valleyrat-campaign.html\"\u003ewhich was first documented by Trend in December 2025\u003c/a\u003e, begins with emails using job opportunities or offers as lures. While Trend reported that the emails featured malicious ZIP or RAR archive attachments, recent examples seen by Symantec were hosted on Dropbox, with the phishing emails likely containing links and instructions to download the file. The attacker may have changed tactics in the belief that downloads from a known cloud service may be less likely to raise red flags than attachments.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf the ZIP archives are opened, they initiate an infection chain leading to the installation of PureRAT or another payload such as a HVNC. \"document.docx\" \u0026gt;nul 2\u0026gt;nul\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"small\"\u003e\u003cem\u003eren \"huna.zip\" \"document.pdf\" \u0026gt;nul 2\u0026gt;nulexit\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"small\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen executed, the batch file creates a hidden directory under %LOCALAPPDATA%\\Google Chrome. It then takes locally saved, innocuously named document.pdf and document.docx files and renames them to huna.zip and huna.exe. The latter is usually a renamed version of 7zip or WinRAR and is used to open huna.zip into the previously created hidden Chrome directory using a hardcoded password (huna@dev.vn). It then runs a Python interpreter (zvhost.exe) from this hidden directory with a Python command that fetches Base64 encoded code for the payload from the following URL: http://196.251.86[.]145/huna2.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAfter decoding and running the payload, it then creates a persistence mechanism by adding itself to the current user’s Run key, ensuring that the same Python script runs at every logon, masquerading as ChromeUpdate. In some cases, a scheduled task is created, with various names, such as 123456.exe.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAfter the persistence mechanism is create it then opens a benign PDF from the same hidden directory, most likely to continue to trick the target into believing they’re simply opening a document. Finally, it renames huna.exe and huna.zip back to document.docx and document.pdf.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eVirtually every step in the batch file has a detailed comment in Vietnamese. This level of commenting is rare outside of scripts authored using AI, particularly in malicious files, which usually contain no comments or minimal comments.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnother version of this batch file had a more streamlined set of instructions and even more evidence of AI assistance.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003cem\u003e@echo off\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003esetlocal enabledelayedexpansion\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003eset \"targetDir=%LOCALAPPDATA%\\Google Chrome\"\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003eset \"exePath=!targetDir!\\zvchost.exe\"\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003eif not exist \"!targetDir!\" (\u003c/em\u003e\u003cbr\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u0026nbsp; The code even contains comments with instructions for the attacker, e.g. “Remember to paste the base64-encoded HVNC shellcode here”.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe attacker appears to be continually refining their attack chain. Multiple variants of scripts were found. Infrastructure also appeared to be regularly chained. In some cases the payload was downloaded from a hardcoded IP address, in others it was downloaded from Gitlab.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eVietnamese threat actor?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAside from the Vietnamese comments in the code, @dev.vn addresses appear in three passwords used by the attackers – the aforementioned “huna@dev.vn”, “hwan@dev.vn\"\u0026nbsp;and “hwanxkiem@dev.vn”. Hwanxkiem appears to be a phonetic variation of Hoàn Kiếm, a district in the Vietnamese capital of Hanoi. One of the file names used by the attacker - nvmeikxnawh.zip – includes the word Hwanxkiem reversed.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe Gitlab account used by the attacker is another variation on the word, in this case with the syllables reversed: gitlab[.]com/kimxhwan. It is unclear what the name and handle on the account (Earlie Waverley and @earliewaverleyfb355) refer to. The name “Huna” is consistently used by the attacker in filenames and password. It doesn’t appear to correspond to any Vietnamese word and may be a handle used by the attacker.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe motivation behind the attacks is more likely to be cybercrime than espionage. The range of organizations targeted and the different lures used suggests a wide ranging trawl rather than targeted activity. The attacker may be casting their net for jobseekers in multiple countries in the hope that they open the emails on their work computer. The malware payloads may then be used to obtain a foothold on these networks in order to sell access on to other attackers.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe use of AI by this attacker is in line with what we see as the primary malicious use case for AI at present. As outlined in\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.security.com/threat-intelligence/ai-whitepaper-research\"\u003eour recent whitepaper\u003c/a\u003e, AI can help lower the barrier to entry for less skilled attackers, helping them to write code and build out attack toolkits.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eProtection/Mitigation\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor the latest protection updates, please visit the \u003ca Investigation by the Symantec and Carbon Black Threat Hunter Team found that this threat is unique and appears to be a completely new ransomware family.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNothing is known yet about who developed Osiris and if it is run as a ransomware-as-a-service (RaaS) but there are some indications that the attackers who used it were previously associated with Inc ransomware. After completing encryption, Osiris appends the .Osiris extension to affected files (e.g. curl.txt becomes curl.txt.Osiris), and deletes snapshots using VSS.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt also terminates the following processes: \u003cem\u003esql; oracle; ocssd; dbsnmp; synctime; agntsvc; isqlplussvc; xfssvccon; mydesktopservice; ocautoupds; encsvc; firefox; tbirdconfig; mydesktopqos; ocomm; dbeng50; sqbcoreservice; excel; infopath; msaccess; mspub; far; onenote; outlook; powerpnt; steam; thebat; thunderbird; visio; winword; wordpad; notepad; wuauclt; onedrive\u003c/em\u003e, and \u003cem\u003esqlmangr\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnd stops the following services: \u003cem\u003evss; sqlsvc; memtas; mepocs; msexchange; Veeam; backup; GxVss; GxBlr; GxCVD,\u003c/em\u003e and \u003cem\u003eGxCIMgr.\u003c/em\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe ransomware uses a hybrid encryption scheme: ECC +AES-128-CTR, and uses a unique AES key for encryption for each file. It also uses completionIOPort to manage asynchronous I/O requests for encryption.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe ransomware also drops a ransom note titled Osiris-MESSAGE.txt, which details the data they claim to have stolen and contains a link to a chat where the victim can negotiate with the attackers.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first suspicious activity occurred on the target’s network a few days before the ransomware was deployed, when Rclone was used to exfiltrate data. This data was exfiltrated to a Wasabi bucket. Wasabi is a legitimate cloud storage service. The tactic of exfiltrating to Wasabi was previously used in October 2025 by Inc ransomware attackers. The overlaps with previous Inc ransomware activity could mean their tactics were being emulated in this attack, or that a former Inc affiliate is now working with or responsible for Osiris. The attackers also used a version of Mimikatz that was previously used, with the same filename (kaz.exe), by attackers deploying Inc, indicating another crossover in activity.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe attackers also deployed other dual-use tools like Netscan, Netexec, and MeshAgent. They also used a custom version of the Rustdesk remote monitoring and management (RMM) tool, which was modified to masquerade its functionality and to include the file description of \"WinZip Remote Desktop\" and the WinZip icon in an effort to hide its true use.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA malicious driver called Abyssworker or Poortry, which masqueraded as a Malwarebytes anti-exploit driver, was also deployed on the target’s network, and was likely used as part of a bring-your-own-vulnerable-driver (BYOVD) attack to disable security software. Poortry\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/hunting-attestation-signed-malware/\"\u003ewas first reported on by Google’s Mandiant investigation team in 2022\u003c/a\u003e. In 2024 and 2025 it was used in attacks attributed to the Medusa ransomware gang. In a Medusa ransomware attack investigated by the Threat Hunter Team in December 2024, a loader called Stonestop was used to load and install Poortry on victim machines and to instruct it as to what actions to perform. Poortry is frequently employed alongside Stonestop.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBYOVD is by far the most frequently used technique for defense impairment by ransomware attackers now. Generally, attackers will deploy a signed vulnerable driver to the target network, which they then exploit to elevate privileges and disable security software. Since the drivers operate with kernel-mode access, they can be used to terminate processes, making them an effective tool for disrupting security measures. Poortry is somewhat unusual because, unlike many drivers, it appears it may have been developed by attackers who then succeeded in getting it signed. In most cases, attackers use a legitimate vulnerable driver to carry out BYOVD attacks, rather than using a custom driver they have developed themselves. KillAV, which is a tool used to deploy vulnerable drivers for terminating security processes, was also deployed on the target’s network. RDP was also enabled on the network, likely to provide the attackers with remote access.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eNew ransomware, old tricksters?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe impact this new Osiris ransomware will have on the ransomware landscape in general remains to be seen. However, it is an effective encryption payload that appears to be wielded by experienced attackers. The use of the Wasabi cloud service and using a version of Mimikatz with the same name as one previously used by Inc ransomware attackers point to potential links with that group or one of its affiliates. Meanwhile, the use of Poortry, previously favored by attackers deploying Medusa, may indicate a link, though Poortry is not believed to be exclusively used by one actor.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWith the constant shifting sands of the ransomware landscape, the emergence of a new ransomware family is always something to keep an eye on.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eFurther reading\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo find out more, read our whitepaper:\u0026nbsp;\u003ca
